</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Born</th>
<td>1098<br/>
<a href="/wiki/Bermersheim_vor_der_H%C3%B6he" title="Bermersheim vor der Höhe">Bermersheim vor der Höhe</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electorate_of_the_Palatinate" title="Electorate of the Palatinate">County Palatine of the Rhine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th scope="row">Died</th>
<td>17 September 1179<span style="display:none">(<span class="dday deathdate">1179-09-17</span>)</span> (aged 81)<br/>
<a href="/wiki/Bingen_am_Rhein" title="Bingen am Rhein">Bingen am Rhein</a>, County Palatine of the Rhine, Holy Roman Empire</td>
</tr>

<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Christian_mysticism" title="Template:Christian mysticism"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Christian_mysticism" title="Template talk:Christian mysticism"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Christian_mysticism&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Hildegard of Bingen</b>, <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Saint_Benedict" title="Order of Saint Benedict">O.S.B.</a> (German: <span lang="de"><i>Hildegard von Bingen</i></span>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <span lang="la"><i>Hildegardis Bingensis</i></span>; 1098 – 17 September 1179), also known as <b>Saint Hildegard</b> and <b><a href="/wiki/Sibyl" title="Sibyl">Sibyl</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Rhine" title="Rhine">Rhine</a></b>, was a German <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benedictine" title="Benedictine">Benedictine</a> <a href="/wiki/Abbess" title="Abbess">abbess</a>, writer, composer, philosopher, <a href="/wiki/Christian_mysticism" title="Christian mysticism">Christian mystic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Visionary" title="Visionary">visionary</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Polymath" title="Polymath">polymath</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bennett.2C_Judith_M_2001_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bennett.2C_Judith_M_2001-1">[1]</a></sup> She is considered to be the founder of scientific <a href="/wiki/Natural_history" title="Natural history">natural history</a> in Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hildegard was elected <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/magistra" title="wiktionary:magistra">magistra</a></i> by her fellow nuns in 1136; she founded the monasteries of <a href="/wiki/Rupertsberg" title="Rupertsberg">Rupertsberg</a> in 1150 and <a href="/wiki/Eibingen" title="Eibingen">Eibingen</a> in 1165. One of her works as a composer, the <i><a href="/wiki/Ordo_Virtutum" title="Ordo Virtutum">Ordo Virtutum</a></i>, is an early example of liturgical drama and arguably the oldest surviving <a href="/wiki/Morality_play" title="Morality play">morality play</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> She wrote theological, botanical, and medicinal texts, as well as letters, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liturgical" title="Liturgical">liturgical</a> songs, and poems, while supervising miniature <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Illumination_(manuscript)" title="Illumination (manuscript)">illuminations</a> in the Rupertsberg manuscript of her first work, <i><a href="/wiki/Scivias" title="Scivias">Scivias</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rupertsberg_MS_images_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rupertsberg_MS_images-4">[4]</a></sup> She is also noted for the invention of a <a href="/wiki/Constructed_language" title="Constructed language">constructed language</a> known as <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_Ignota" title="Lingua Ignota">Lingua Ignota</a></i>.</p>
<p>Although the history of her formal consideration is complicated, she has been recognized as a saint by branches of the Roman Catholic Church for centuries. On 7 October 2012, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Benedict_XVI" title="Pope Benedict XVI">Pope Benedict XVI</a> named her a <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_the_Church" title="Doctor of the Church">Doctor of the Church</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biography">Biography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hildegard_of_Bingen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Biography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Hildegard was born around the year 1098, although the exact date is uncertain. Her parents were Mechtild of Merxheim-Nahet and Hildebert of Bermersheim, a family of the free lower nobility in the service of the Count Meginhard of <a href="/wiki/County_of_Sponheim" title="County of Sponheim">Sponheim</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Sickly from birth, Hildegard is traditionally considered their youngest and tenth child,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> although there are records of seven older siblings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> In her <i>Vita</i>, Hildegard states that from a very young age she had experienced <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vision_(religion)" title="Vision (religion)">visions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruether.2C_Rosemary_Radford_2002_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruether.2C_Rosemary_Radford_2002-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monastic_life">Monastic life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hildegard_of_Bingen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Monastic life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Perhaps because of Hildegard's visions, or as a method of political positioning (or both), Hildegard's parents offered her as an <a href="/wiki/Oblate" title="Oblate">oblate</a> to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Order_of_St._Benedict" title="Order of St. Benedict">Benedictine</a> monastery at the <a href="/wiki/Disibodenberg" title="Disibodenberg">Disibodenberg</a>, which had been recently reformed in the <a href="/wiki/Palatinate_Forest" title="Palatinate Forest">Palatinate Forest</a>. The date of Hildegard's enclosure at the monastery is the subject of debate. Her <i><a href="/wiki/Hagiography" title="Hagiography">Vita</a></i> says she was professed with an older woman, <a href="/wiki/Jutta_von_Sponheim" title="Jutta von Sponheim">Jutta</a>, the daughter of Count Stephan II of Sponheim, at the age of eight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> However, Jutta's date of enclosure is known to have been in 1112, when Hildegard would have been fourteen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Their vows were received by Bishop Otto Bamberg on All Saints' Day, 1112. Some scholars speculate that Hildegard was placed in the care of Jutta at the age of eight, and the two women were then enclosed together six years later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>In any case, Hildegard and Jutta were enclosed together at the Disibodenberg, and formed the core of a growing community of women attached to the male monastery. Jutta was also a visionary and thus attracted many followers who came to visit her at the cloister. Hildegard tells us that Jutta taught her to read and write, but that she was unlearned and therefore incapable of teaching Hildegard sound biblical interpretation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> The written record of the <i>Life of Jutta</i> indicates that Hildegard probably assisted her in reciting the psalms, working in the garden and other handiwork, and tending to the sick.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> This might have been a time when Hildegard learned how to play the ten-stringed <a href="/wiki/Psaltery" title="Psaltery">psaltery</a>. <a href="/wiki/Volmar_(monk)" title="Volmar (monk)">Volmar</a>, a frequent visitor, may have taught Hildegard simple psalm notation. The time she studied music could have been the beginning of the compositions she would later create.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Upon Jutta's death in 1136, Hildegard was unanimously elected as <i>magistra</i> of the community by her fellow nuns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Abbot Kuno of Disibodenberg asked Hildegard to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prioress" title="Prioress">Prioress</a>, which would be under his authority. Hildegard, however, wanted more independence for herself and her nuns, and asked Abbot Kuno to allow them to move to <a href="/wiki/Rupertsberg" title="Rupertsberg">Rupertsberg</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> This was to be a move towards poverty, from a stone complex that was well established to a temporary dwelling place. When the abbot declined Hildegard's proposition, Hildegard went over his head and received the approval of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archbishop_Henry_I_of_Mainz" title="Archbishop Henry I of Mainz">Archbishop Henry I of Mainz</a>. Abbot Kuno did not relent until Hildegard was stricken by an illness that kept her paralyzed and unable to move from her bed, an event that she attributed to God's unhappiness at her not following his orders to move her nuns to Rupertsberg. It was only when the Abbot himself could not move Hildegard that he decided to grant the nuns their own monastery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Hildegard and about twenty nuns thus moved to the St. Rupertsberg monastery in 1150, where <a href="/wiki/Volmar_(monk)" title="Volmar (monk)">Volmar</a> served as provost, as well as Hildegard's confessor and scribe. In 1165 Hildegard founded a second monastery for her nuns at <a href="/wiki/Eibingen_Abbey" title="Eibingen Abbey">Eibingen</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Visions">Visions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hildegard_of_Bingen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Visions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Hildegard says that she first saw "The Shade of the Living Light" at the age of three, and by the age of five she began to understand that she was experiencing visions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> She used the term 'visio' to this feature of her experience, and recognized that it was a gift that she could not explain to others. Hildegard explained that she saw all things in the light of God through the five senses: sight, hearing, taste, smell, and touch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Hildegard was hesitant to share her visions, confiding only to <a href="/wiki/Jutta_von_Sponheim" title="Jutta von Sponheim">Jutta</a>, who in turn told Volmar, Hildegard's tutor and, later, secretary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Throughout her life, she continued to have many visions, and in 1141, at the age of 42, Hildegard received a vision she believed to be an instruction from God, to "write down that which you see and hear."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Still hesitant to record her visions, Hildegard became physically ill. The illustrations recorded in the book of Scivias were visions that Hildegard experienced, causing her great suffering and tribulations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> In her first theological text, <i><a href="/wiki/Scivias" title="Scivias">Scivias</a></i> ("Know the Ways"), Hildegard describes her struggle within:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>But I, though I saw and heard these things, refused to write for a long time through doubt and bad opinion and the diversity of human words, not with stubbornness but in the exercise of humility, until, laid low by the scourge of God, I fell upon a bed of sickness; then, compelled at last by many illnesses, and by the witness of a certain noble maiden of good conduct [the nun Richardis von Stade] and of that man whom I had secretly sought and found, as mentioned above, I set my hand to the writing. While I was doing it, I sensed, as I mentioned before, the deep profundity of scriptural exposition; and, raising myself from illness by the strength I received, I brought this work to a close – though just barely – in ten years. (...) And I spoke and wrote these things not by the invention of my heart or that of any other person, but as by the secret mysteries of God I heard and received them in the heavenly places. And again I heard a voice from Heaven saying to me, 'Cry out therefore, and write thus!'<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>It was between November 1147 and February 1148 at the synod in Trier that <a href="/wiki/Pope_Eugene_III" title="Pope Eugene III">Pope Eugenius</a> heard about Hildegard’s writings. It was from this that she received Papal approval to document her visions as revelations from the Holy Spirit giving her instant credence.</p>
<p>Before Hildegard’s death, a problem arose with the clergy of Mainz. A man buried in Rupertsburg had died after excommunication from the Church. Therefore, the clergy wanted to remove his body from the sacred ground. Hildegard did not accept this idea, replying that it was a sin and that the man had been reconciled to the church at the time of his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 17 September 1179, when Hildegard died, her sisters claimed they saw two streams of light appear in the skies and cross over the room where she was dying.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Madigan.2C_Shawn_1998_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Madigan.2C_Shawn_1998-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vita_Sanctae_Hildegardis"><i>Vita Sanctae Hildegardis</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hildegard_of_Bingen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Vita Sanctae Hildegardis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Hildegard's <a href="/wiki/Hagiography" title="Hagiography">hagiography</a>, <i>Vita Sanctae Hildegardis</i>, was compiled by the monk Theoderic of Echternach after Hildegard's death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> He included the hagiographical work <i>Libellus</i> or "Little Book" begun by Godfrey of Disibodenberg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Godfrey had died before he was able to complete his work. Guibert of Gembloux was invited to finish the work; however, he had to return to his monastery with the project unfinished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Theoderic utilized sources Guibert had left behind to complete the <i>Vita</i>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Works">Works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hildegard_of_Bingen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:07angels-hildegard_von_bingen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4085" data-file-width="3487" height="351" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/07angels-hildegard_von_bingen.jpg/300px-07angels-hildegard_von_bingen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/07angels-hildegard_von_bingen.jpg/450px-07angels-hildegard_von_bingen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/07angels-hildegard_von_bingen.jpg/600px-07angels-hildegard_von_bingen.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:07angels-hildegard_von_bingen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Scivias</i> I.6: The Choirs of Angels. From <a class="external text" href="http://www.abtei-st-hildegard.de/?portfolio=der-rupertsberger-%E2%80%9Escivias-kodex" rel="nofollow">the Rupertsberg manuscript</a>, fol. 38r.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hildegard's works include three great volumes of visionary theology;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> a variety of musical compositions for use in liturgy, as well as the musical morality play <i>Ordo Virtutum</i>; one of the largest bodies of letters (nearly 400) to survive from the Middle Ages, addressed to correspondents ranging from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Popes" title="Popes">popes</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperors" title="Holy Roman Emperors">emperors</a> to <a href="/wiki/Abbot" title="Abbot">abbots</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abbess" title="Abbess">abbesses</a>, and including records of many of the sermons she preached in the 1160s and 1170s;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> two volumes of material on natural medicine and cures;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glaze_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Glaze-33">[33]</a></sup> an invented language called the <a href="/wiki/Lingua_Ignota" title="Lingua Ignota"><i>Lingua ignota</i></a> ("unknown language");<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> and various minor works, including a gospel commentary and two works of hagiography.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>Several manuscripts of her works were produced during her lifetime, including the illustrated Rupertsberg manuscript of her first major work, <i><a href="/wiki/Scivias" title="Scivias">Scivias</a></i> (lost since 1945); the Dendermonde manuscript, which contains one version of her musical works; and the Ghent manuscript, which was the first fair-copy made for editing of her final theological work, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liber_Divinorum_Operum" title="Liber Divinorum Operum">Liber Divinorum Operum</a></i>. At the end of her life, and probably under her initial guidance, all of her works were edited and gathered into the single <a class="external text" href="http://www.hs-rm.de/hlb/suchen-finden/sondersammlungen/handschriften-inkunabeln-alte-drucke/der-riesencodex-hildegard-von-bingen/index.html" rel="nofollow">Riesenkodex manuscript</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Visionary_theology">Visionary theology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hildegard_of_Bingen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Visionary theology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Hildegard's most significant works were her three volumes of visionary theology: <i><a href="/wiki/Scivias" title="Scivias">Scivias</a></i> ("Know the Ways", composed 1142-1151), <i>Liber Vitae Meritorum</i> ("Book of Life's Merits" or "Book of the Rewards of Life", composed 1158-1163); and <i>Liber Divinorum Operum</i> ("Book of Divine Works", also known as <i>De operatione Dei</i>, "On God's Activity", composed 1163/4-1172 or 1174). In these volumes, the last of which was completed when she was well into her seventies, Hildegard first describes each vision, whose details are often strange and enigmatic; and then interprets their theological contents in the words of the "voice of the Living Light."</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Scivias"><i>Scivias</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hildegard_of_Bingen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Scivias">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Meister_des_Hildegardis-Codex_004.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1006" data-file-width="776" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Meister_des_Hildegardis-Codex_004.jpg/200px-Meister_des_Hildegardis-Codex_004.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Meister_des_Hildegardis-Codex_004.jpg/300px-Meister_des_Hildegardis-Codex_004.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Meister_des_Hildegardis-Codex_004.jpg/400px-Meister_des_Hildegardis-Codex_004.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Meister_des_Hildegardis-Codex_004.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Church, the Bride of Christ and Mother of the Faithful in Baptism. Illustration to <i>Scivias</i> II.3, fol. 51r from the 20th-century facsimile of the Rupertsberg manuscript, c. 1165-1180</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The composition of the first work, <i><a href="/wiki/Scivias" title="Scivias">Scivias</a></i>, was triggered by the insistence of her visionary experiences in about 1142, when she was already forty-three years old. Perceiving a divine command to "write down what you see and hear",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Hildegard began to record her visionary experiences. <i><a href="/wiki/Scivias" title="Scivias">Scivias</a></i> is structured into three parts of unequal length. The first part (six visions) chronicles the order of God's creation: the Creation and Fall of Adam and Eve, the structure of the universe (famously described as an "egg"), the relationship between body and soul, God's relationship to his people through the Synagogue, and the choirs of angels. The second part (seven visions) describes the order of redemption: the coming of Christ the Redeemer, the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a>, the Church as the Bride of Christ and the Mother of the Faithful in <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Confirmation" title="Confirmation">confirmation</a>, the orders of the Church, Christ's sacrifice on the Cross and the <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">Eucharist</a>, and the fight against the devil. Finally, the third part (thirteen visions) recapitulates the history of salvation told in the first two parts, symbolized as a building adorned with various allegorical figures and virtues. It concludes with the Symphony of Heaven, an early version of Hildegard's musical compositions.</p>
<p>Portions of the uncompleted work were read aloud to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pope_Eugenius_III" title="Pope Eugenius III">Pope Eugenius III</a> at the Synod of Trier in 1148, after which he sent Hildegard a letter with his blessing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> This blessing was later construed as papal approval for all of Hildegard's wide-ranging theological activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Towards the end of her life, Hildegard commissioned a richly decorated manuscript of <i><a href="/wiki/Scivias" title="Scivias">Scivias</a></i> (the Rupertsberg Codex); although the original has been lost since its evacuation to Dresden for safekeeping in 1945, its images are preserved in a hand-painted facsimile from the 1920s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rupertsberg_MS_images_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rupertsberg_MS_images-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Liber_Vitae_Meritorum"><i>Liber Vitae Meritorum</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hildegard_of_Bingen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Liber Vitae Meritorum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In her second volume of visionary theology, composed between 1158 and 1163, after she had moved her community of nuns into independence at the Rupertsberg in Bingen, Hildegard tackled the moral life in the form of dramatic confrontations between the virtues and the vices. She had already explored this area in her musical morality play, <i>Ordo Virtutum</i>, and the "Book of the Rewards of Life" takes up that play's characteristic themes. Each vice, although ultimately depicted as ugly and grotesque, nevertheless offers alluring, seductive speeches that attempt to entice the unwary soul into their clutches. Standing in our defense, however, are the sober voices of the Virtues, powerfully confronting every vicious deception.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>Amongst the work's innovations is one of the earliest descriptions of purgatory as the place where each soul would have to work off its debts after death before entering heaven.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Hildegard's descriptions of the possible punishments there are often gruesome and grotesque, which emphasize the work's moral and pastoral purpose as a practical guide to the life of true penance and proper virtue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Liber_Divinorum_Operum"><i>Liber Divinorum Operum</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hildegard_of_Bingen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Liber Divinorum Operum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hildegard_von_Bingen_Liber_Divinorum_Operum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1133" data-file-width="768" height="295" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Hildegard_von_Bingen_Liber_Divinorum_Operum.jpg/200px-Hildegard_von_Bingen_Liber_Divinorum_Operum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Hildegard_von_Bingen_Liber_Divinorum_Operum.jpg/300px-Hildegard_von_Bingen_Liber_Divinorum_Operum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Hildegard_von_Bingen_Liber_Divinorum_Operum.jpg/400px-Hildegard_von_Bingen_Liber_Divinorum_Operum.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hildegard_von_Bingen_Liber_Divinorum_Operum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"Universal Man" illumination from Hildegard's <i>Liber Divinorum Operum</i>, I.2. Lucca, MS 1942, early 13th-century copy.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hildegard's last and grandest visionary work had its genesis in one of the few times she experienced something like an ecstatic loss of consciousness. As she described it in an autobiographical passage included in her Vita, sometime in about 1163, she received "an extraordinary mystical vision" in which was revealed the "sprinkling drops of sweet rain" that John the Evangelist experienced when he wrote, "In the beginning was the Word..." (John 1:1). Hildegard perceived that this Word was the key to the "Work of God", of which humankind is the pinnacle. The "Book of Divine Works", therefore, became in many ways an extended explication of the Prologue to John's Gospel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>The ten visions of this work's three parts are cosmic in scale, often populated by the grand allegorical female figures representing Divine Love (<i>Caritas</i>) or Wisdom (<i>Sapientia</i>). The first of these opens the work with a salvo of poetic and visionary images, swirling about to characterize the dynamic activity of God within the scope of his salvation-historical work. The remaining three visions of the first part introduce the famous image of a human being standing astride the spheres that make up the universe, and detail the intricate relationships between the human as microcosm and the universe as macrocosm. This culminates in the final chapters of Part One, Vision Four with Hildegard's direct rumination on the meaning of "In the beginning was the Word..." (John 1:1). The single vision that comprises the whole of Part Two stretches that rumination back to the opening of Genesis, and forms an extended meditation on the six days of the creation of the world. Finally, the five visions of the third part take up again the building imagery of <i>Scivias</i> to describe the course of salvation history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hildegard_of_Bingen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Attention in recent decades to women of the medieval <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Church</a> has led to a great deal of popular interest in Hildegard's music. In addition to the <i><a href="/wiki/Ordo_Virtutum" title="Ordo Virtutum">Ordo Virtutum</a>,</i> sixty-nine musical compositions, each with its own original poetic text, survive, and at least four other texts are known, though their musical notation has been lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> This is one of the largest repertoires among medieval composers.</p>
<table class="mbox-small" role="presentation" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;">
<tr>

<div class="center">

</tr>
<tr>
<td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em"><span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>

</table>
<p>One of her better known works, <i>Ordo Virtutum</i> (<i>Play of the Virtues</i>), is a <a href="/wiki/Morality_play" title="Morality play">morality play</a>. It is uncertain when some of Hildegard’s compositions were composed, though the <i>Ordo Virtutum</i> is thought to have been composed as early as 1151.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> The morality play consists of <a href="/wiki/Monophony" title="Monophony">monophonic</a> melodies for the Anima (human soul) and 16 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virtues" title="Virtues">Virtues</a>. There is also one speaking part for the Devil. Scholars assert that the role of the Devil would have been played by Volmar, while Hildegard's nuns would have played the parts of Anima and the Virtues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to the <i>Ordo Virtutum</i> Hildegard composed many liturgical songs that were collected into a cycle called the <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Symphonia_armoniae_celestium_revelationum&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Symphonia armoniae celestium revelationum (page does not exist)">Symphonia armoniae celestium revelationum</a>.</i> The songs from the Symphonia are set to Hildegard's own text and range from antiphons, hymns, and sequences, to responsories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Her music is described as <a href="/wiki/Monophony" title="Monophony">monophonic</a>, that is, consisting of exactly one melodic line.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Its style is characterized by soaring melodies that can push the boundaries of the more staid ranges of traditional Gregorian chant. Though Hildegard's music is often thought to stand outside the normal practices of monophonic monastic chant,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-holsinger_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-holsinger-50">[50]</a></sup> current researchers are also exploring ways in which it may be viewed in comparison with her contemporaries, such as Hermannus Contractus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Another feature of Hildegard's music that both reflects twelfth-century evolutions of chant and pushes those evolutions further is that it is highly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Melismatic" title="Melismatic">melismatic</a>, often with recurrent melodic units. Scholars such as Margot Fassler, Marianne Richert Pfau, and Beverly Lomer also note the intimate relationship between music and text in Hildegard's compositions, whose rhetorical features are often more distinct than is common in twelfth-century chant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> As with all medieval chant notation, Hildegard's music lacks any indication of tempo or rhythm; the surviving manuscripts employ late German style notation, which uses very ornamental <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neumes" title="Neumes">neumes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The reverence for the Virgin Mary reflected in music shows how deeply influenced and inspired Hildegard of Bingen and her community were by the Virgin Mary and the saints.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>The definition of <a href="/wiki/Viriditas" title="Viriditas">viriditas</a> or "greenness" is an earthly expression of the heavenly in an integrity that overcomes dualisms. This greenness or power of life appears frequently in Hildegard's works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite Hildegard's self-professed view that her compositions have as object the praise of God, one scholar has asserted that Hildegard made a close association between music and the female body in her musical compositions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-holsinger_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-holsinger-50">[50]</a></sup> According to him, the poetry and music of Hildegard’s Symphonia would therefore be concerned with the anatomy of female desire thus described as Sapphonic, or pertaining to <a href="/wiki/Sappho" title="Sappho">Sappho</a>, connecting her to a history of female rhetoricians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_and_medicinal_writings">Scientific and medicinal writings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hildegard_of_Bingen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Scientific and medicinal writings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Hildegard’s medicinal and scientific writings, though thematically complementary to her ideas about nature expressed in her visionary works, are different in focus and scope. Neither claim to be rooted in her visionary experience and its divine authority. Rather, they spring from her experience helping in and then leading the monastery’s herbal garden and infirmary, as well as the theoretical information she likely gained through her wide-ranging reading in the monastery’s library.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glaze_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Glaze-33">[33]</a></sup> As she gained practical skills in diagnosis, prognosis, and treatment, she combined physical treatment of physical diseases with holistic methods centered on “spiritual healing.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweet_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sweet-57">[57]</a></sup> She became well known for her healing powers involving practical application of tinctures, herbs, and precious stones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> She combined these elements with a theological notion ultimately derived from Genesis: all things put on earth are for the use of humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hildegard catalogued both her theory and practice in two works. The first, <i>Physica,</i> contains nine books that describe the scientific and medicinal properties of various plants, stones, fish, reptiles, and animals. The second, <i>Causae et Curae</i>, is an exploration of the human body, its connections to the rest of the natural world, and the causes and cures of various diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-medical_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-medical-60">[60]</a></sup> Hildegard documented various medical practices in these books, including the use of bleeding and home remedies for many common ailments. She also explains remedies for common agricultural injuries such as burns, fractures, dislocations, and cuts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweet_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sweet-57">[57]</a></sup> Hildegard may have used the books to teach assistants at the monastery. These books are historically significant because they show areas of medieval medicine that were not well documented because their practitioners (mainly women) rarely wrote in Latin. Her writings were commentated on by <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A9lanie_Lipinska" title="Mélanie Lipinska">Mélanie Lipinska</a>, a Polish scientist<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to its wealth of practical evidence, <i>Causae et Curae</i> is also noteworthy for its organizational scheme. Its first part sets the work within the context of the creation of the cosmos and then humanity as its summit, and the constant interplay of the human person as microcosm both physically and spiritually with the macrocosm of the universe informs all of Hildegard’s approach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glaze_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-Glaze-33">[33]</a></sup> Her hallmark is to emphasize the vital connection between the “green” health of the natural world and the holistic health of the human person. Thus, when she approached medicine as a type of gardening, it was not just as an analogy. Rather, Hildegard understood the plants and elements of the garden as direct counterparts to the humors and elements within the human body, whose imbalance led to illness and disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweet_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sweet-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>Thus, the nearly three hundred chapters of the second book of <i>Causae et Curae</i> “explore the etiology, or causes, of disease as well as human sexuality, psychology, and physiology.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glaze_33-3"><a href="#cite_note-Glaze-33">[33]</a></sup> In this section, she give specific instructions for bleeding based on various factors, including gender, the phase of the moon (bleeding is best done when moon is waning), the place of disease (use veins near diseased organ of body part) or prevention (big veins in arms), and how much blood to take (described in imprecise measurements, like “the amount that a thirsty person can swallow in one gulp”). She even includes bleeding instructions for animals to keep them healthy. In the third and fourth sections, Hildegard describes treatments for malignant and minor problems and diseases according to the humoral theory, again including information on animal health. The fifth section is about diagnosis and prognosis, which includes instructions to check the patient’s blood, pulse, urine and stool.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweet_57-3"><a href="#cite_note-Sweet-57">[57]</a></sup> Finally, the sixth section documents a lunar horoscope to provide an additional means of prognosis for both disease and other medical conditions, such as conception and the outcome of pregnancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glaze_33-4"><a href="#cite_note-Glaze-33">[33]</a></sup> For example, she indicates that a waxing moon is good for human conception and is also good for sowing seeds for plants (sowing seeds is the plant equivalent of conception).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweet_57-4"><a href="#cite_note-Sweet-57">[57]</a></sup> Elsewhere, Hildegard is even said to have stressed the value of boiling drinking water in an attempt to prevent infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encyclopedia_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-encyclopedia-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>As Hildegard elaborates the medical and scientific relationship between the human microcosm and the macrocosm of the universe, she often focuses on interrelated patterns of four: “the four elements (fire, air, water, and earth), the four seasons, the four humors, the four zones of the earth, and the four major winds.”<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glaze_33-5"><a href="#cite_note-Glaze-33">[33]</a></sup> Although she inherited the basic framework of <a href="/wiki/Humorism" title="Humorism">humoral theory</a> from ancient medicine, Hildegard’s conception of the hierarchical inter-balance of the four humors (blood, phlegm, black bile, and yellow bile) was unique, based on their correspondence to “superior” and “inferior” elements—blood and phlegm corresponding to the “celestial” elements of fire and air, and the two biles corresponding to the “terrestrial” elements of water and earth. Hildegard understood the disease-causing imbalance of these humors to result from the improper dominance of the subordinate humors. This disharmony reflects that introduced by Adam and Eve in the Fall, which for Hildegard marked the indelible entrance of disease and humoral imbalance into humankind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Glaze_33-6"><a href="#cite_note-Glaze-33">[33]</a></sup> As she writes in <i>Causae et Curae</i> c. 42:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>It happens that certain men suffer diverse illnesses. This comes from the phlegm which is superabundant within them. For if man had remained in paradise, he would not have had the <i>flegmata</i> within his body, from which many evils proceed, but his flesh would have been whole and without dark humor [<i>livor</i>]. However, because he consented to evil and relinquished good, he was made into a likeness of the earth, which produces good and useful herbs, as well as bad and useless ones, and which has in itself both good and evil moistures. From tasting evil, the blood of the sons of Adam was turned into the poison of semen, out of which the sons of man are begotten. And therefore their flesh is ulcerated and permeable [to disease]. These sores and openings create a certain storm and smoky moisture in men, from which the <i>flegmata</i> arise and coagulate, which then introduce diverse infirmities to the human body. All this arose from the first evil, which man began at the start, because if Adam had remained in paradise, he would have had the sweetest health, and the best dwelling-place, just as the strongest balsam emits the best odor; but on the contrary, man now has within himself poison and phlegm and diverse illnesses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lingua_Ignota_and_invented_alphabet"><i>Lingua Ignota</i> and invented alphabet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hildegard_of_Bingen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Lingua Ignota and invented alphabet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Litterae_ignotae.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="121" data-file-width="352" height="69" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Litterae_ignotae.png/200px-Litterae_ignotae.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Litterae_ignotae.png/300px-Litterae_ignotae.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/04/Litterae_ignotae.png 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Litterae_ignotae.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Alphabet by Hildegard von Bingen, Litterae ignotae, which she used for her language <a href="/wiki/Lingua_Ignota" title="Lingua Ignota">Lingua Ignota</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hildegard also invented an <a href="/wiki/Constructed_script" title="Constructed script">alternative alphabet</a>. The text of her writing and compositions reveals Hildegard's use of this form of modified medieval <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a>, encompassing many invented, conflated and abridged words.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruether.2C_Rosemary_Radford_2002_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ruether.2C_Rosemary_Radford_2002-9">[9]</a></sup> Because of her inventions of words for her lyrics and use of a constructed script, many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conlang" title="Conlang">conlangers</a> look upon her as a medieval precursor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Scholars believe that Hildegard used her <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_Ignota" title="Lingua Ignota">Lingua Ignota</a></i> to increase solidarity among her nuns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Significance">Significance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hildegard_of_Bingen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Significance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="During_her_lifetime">During her lifetime</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hildegard_of_Bingen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: During her lifetime">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Maddocks claims that it is likely Hildegard learned simple Latin and the tenets of the Christian faith but was not instructed in the Seven <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liberal_Arts" title="Liberal Arts">Liberal Arts</a>, which formed the basis of all education for the learned classes in the Middle Ages: the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trivium_(education)" title="Trivium (education)">Trivium</a></i> of grammar, dialectic, and rhetoric plus the <i><a href="/wiki/Quadrivium" title="Quadrivium">Quadrivium</a></i> of arithmetic, geometry, astronomy, and music.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The correspondence she kept with the outside world, both spiritual and social, transcended the cloister as a space of spiritual confinement and served to document Hildegard’s grand style and strict formatting of medieval letter writing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dietrich.2C_Julia_1997_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dietrich.2C_Julia_1997-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>Contributing to Christian European rhetorical traditions, Hildegard "authorized herself as a theologian" through alternative rhetorical arts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dietrich.2C_Julia_1997_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Dietrich.2C_Julia_1997-67">[67]</a></sup> Hildegard was creative in her interpretation of theology. She believed that her monastery should exclude novices who were not from the nobility because she did not want her community to be divided on the basis of social status.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> She also stated that "woman may be made from man, but no man can be made without a woman."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Madigan.2C_Shawn_1998_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Madigan.2C_Shawn_1998-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hildegard_map.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="619" data-file-width="495" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Hildegard_map.jpg/200px-Hildegard_map.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Hildegard_map.jpg/300px-Hildegard_map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Hildegard_map.jpg/400px-Hildegard_map.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hildegard_map.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hildegard's preaching tours</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Because of church limitation on public, discursive rhetoric, the medieval rhetorical arts included preaching, letter writing, poetry, and the encyclopedic tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Hildegard’s participation in these arts speaks to her significance as a female rhetorician, transcending bans on women's social participation and interpretation of scripture. The acceptance of public preaching by a woman, even a well-connected abbess and acknowledged prophet, does not fit the stereotype of this time. Her preaching was not limited to the monasteries; she preached publicly in 1160 in Germany. (New York: Routledge, 2001, 9). She conducted four preaching tours throughout Germany, speaking to both clergy and laity in chapter houses and in public, mainly denouncing clerical corruption and calling for reform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many abbots and abbesses asked her for prayers and opinions on various matters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bennett.2C_Judith_M_2001_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bennett.2C_Judith_M_2001-1">[1]</a></sup> She traveled widely during her four preaching tours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> She had several fanatical followers, including Guibert of Gembloux, who wrote to her frequently and became her secretary after Volmar's death in 1173. Hildegard also influenced several monastic women, exchanging letters with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elizabeth_of_Sch%C3%B6nau" title="Elizabeth of Schönau">Elisabeth of Schönau</a>, a nearby visionary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hildegard corresponded with popes such as <a href="/wiki/Pope_Eugene_III" title="Pope Eugene III">Eugene III</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anastasius_IV" title="Anastasius IV">Anastasius IV</a>, statesmen such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abbot_Suger" title="Abbot Suger">Abbot Suger</a>, German emperors such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frederick_I_Barbarossa" title="Frederick I Barbarossa">Frederick I Barbarossa</a>, and other notable figures such as Saint <a href="/wiki/Bernard_of_Clairvaux" title="Bernard of Clairvaux">Bernard of Clairvaux</a>, who advanced her work, at the behest of her abbot, Kuno, at the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Synod_of_Trier&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Synod of Trier (page does not exist)">Synod of Trier</a> in 1147 and 1148. Hildegard of Bingen's correspondence is an important component of her literary output.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beatification.2C_canonization_and_recognition_as_a_Doctor_of_the_Church">Beatification, canonization and recognition as a Doctor of the Church</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hildegard_of_Bingen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Beatification, canonization and recognition as a Doctor of the Church">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Hildegard was one of the first persons for whom the Roman <a href="/wiki/Canonization" title="Canonization">canonization</a> process was officially applied, but the process took so long that four attempts at canonization were not completed and she remained at the level of her <a href="/wiki/Beatification" title="Beatification">beatification</a>. Her name was nonetheless taken up in the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Martyrology" title="Roman Martyrology">Roman Martyrology</a> at the end of the 16th century. Her <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feast_day" title="Feast day">feast day</a> is 17 September. Numerous popes have referred to Hildegard as a saint, including <a href="/wiki/Pope_John_Paul_II" title="Pope John Paul II">Pope John Paul II</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Pope_Benedict_XVI" title="Pope Benedict XVI">Pope Benedict XVI</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 10 May 2012, Pope Benedict XVI extended the liturgical cult of St. Hildegard to the entire Catholic Church<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> in a process known as "equivalent canonization,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> thus laying the groundwork for naming her a <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_the_Church" title="Doctor of the Church">Doctor of the Church</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> On 7 October 2012, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feast_of_the_Holy_Rosary" title="Feast of the Holy Rosary">feast of the Holy Rosary</a>, the pope named her a <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_the_Church" title="Doctor of the Church">Doctor of the Church</a>, the fourth woman of 35 saints given that title by the Roman Catholic Church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> He called her "perennially relevant" and "an authentic teacher of theology and a profound scholar of natural science and music."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hildegard of Bingen also appears in the <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints" title="Calendar of saints">calendar of saints</a> of various <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion" title="Anglican Communion">Anglican</a> churches, such as that of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>, in which she is commemorated on 17 September.</p>
<p>Hildegard's parish and pilgrimage church in Eibingen near <a href="/wiki/R%C3%BCdesheim_am_Rhein" title="Rüdesheim am Rhein">Rüdesheim</a> houses her <a href="/wiki/Relic" title="Relic">relics</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_interest">Modern interest</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hildegard_of_Bingen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Modern interest">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hildegard_VS.JPG"><img alt="German 10 DM commemorative coin issued by the Federal Republic of Germany (1998): Hildegard of Bingen writing the book (Liber), 'Sci vias Domini', inspired by the hand of the Lord" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1953" data-file-width="2004" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Hildegard_VS.JPG/150px-Hildegard_VS.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Hildegard_VS.JPG/225px-Hildegard_VS.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Hildegard_VS.JPG/300px-Hildegard_VS.JPG 2x" width="150"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hildegard_VS.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
German 10 DM commemorative coin issued by the Federal Republic of Germany (1998) designed by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Vezerfi-Clemm" title="Carl Vezerfi-Clemm">Carl Vezerfi-Clemm</a> on the 900th anniversary of Hildegard of Bingen's birth</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In recent years, Hildegard has become of particular interest to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feminist" title="Feminist">feminist</a> scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> They note her reference to herself as a member of the "weaker sex" and her rather constant belittling of women. Hildegard frequently referred to herself as an unlearned woman, completely incapable of Biblical exegesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Such a statement on her part, however, worked to her advantage because it made her statements that all of her writings and music came from visions of the Divine more believable, therefore giving Hildegard the authority to speak in a time and place where few women were permitted a voice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Hildegard used her voice to amplify the Church's condemnation of institutional corruption, in particular <a href="/wiki/Simony" title="Simony">simony</a>.</p>
<p>Hildegard has also become a figure of reverence within the contemporary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Age_movement" title="New Age movement">New Age movement</a>, mostly because of her holistic and natural view of healing, as well as her status as a mystic. Though her medical writings were long neglected, and then studied without reference to their context,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweet4_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sweet4-85">[85]</a></sup> she was the inspiration for Dr. Gottfried Hertzka's "Hildegard-Medicine", and is the namesake for June Boyce-Tillman's Hildegard Network, a healing center that focuses on a holistic approach to wellness and brings together people interested in exploring the links between spirituality, the arts, and healing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Her reputation as a medicinal writer and healer was also used by early feminists to argue for women's rights to attend medical schools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sweet4_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sweet4-85">[85]</a></sup> Hildegard's reincarnation has been debated since 1924 when Austrian mystic <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Steiner" title="Rudolf Steiner">Rudolf Steiner</a> lectured that a nun of her description was the past life of Russian poet philosopher <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Solovyov_(philosopher)" title="Vladimir Solovyov (philosopher)">Vladimir Soloviev</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> whose <a href="/wiki/Sophia_(wisdom)" title="Sophia (wisdom)">Sophianic</a> visions are often compared to Hildegard's.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Sophiologist Robert Powell writes that hermetic astrology proves the match,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> while mystical communities in Hildegard's lineage include that of artist Carl Schroeder<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> as studied by Columbia sociologist Courtney Bender<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> and supported by reincarnation researchers Walter Semkiw and Kevin Ryerson.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>Recordings and performances of Hildegard's music have gained critical praise and popularity since 1979. See Discography listed below.</p>
<p>The following modern musical works are directly linked to Hildegard and her music or texts:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sofia_Gubaidulina" title="Sofia Gubaidulina">Sofia Gubaidulina</a>: <i>Aus den Visionen der Hildegard von Bingen</i>, for contra alto solo, after a text of Hildegard of Bingen, 1994.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Peter_Janssens" title="Peter Janssens">Peter Janssens</a>: <i>Hildegard von Bingen</i>, a musical in 10 scenes, text: <a href="/wiki/Jutta_Richter" title="Jutta Richter">Jutta Richter</a>, 1997.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tilo_Medek" title="Tilo Medek">Tilo Medek</a>: <i>Monatsbilder (nach Hildegard von Bingen)</i>, twelve songs for mezzo-soprano, clarinet and piano, 1997.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/David_Lynch" title="David Lynch">David Lynch</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jocelyn_Montgomery" title="Jocelyn Montgomery">Jocelyn Montgomery</a>: <i>Lux Vivens (Living Light): The Music of Hildegard Von Bingen</i>, 1998.</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Alois_Albrecht&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Alois Albrecht (page does not exist)">Alois Albrecht</a>: <i>Hildegard von Bingen</i>, a liturgical play with texts and music by Hildegard of Bingen, 1998.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Christopher_Theofanidis" title="Christopher Theofanidis">Christopher Theofanidis</a>: <i><a href="/wiki/Rainbow_Body" title="Rainbow Body">Rainbow Body</a></i>, for orchestra (2000)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ludger_St%C3%BChlmeyer" title="Ludger Stühlmeyer">Ludger Stühlmeyer</a>: <i>O splendidissima gemma</i>, for alto solo and organ, text by Hildegard of Bingen, 2011.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Sauseng" title="Wolfgang Sauseng">Wolfgang Sauseng</a>: <i>De visione secunda</i> for double choir and percussion, 2011.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Devendra_Banhart" title="Devendra Banhart">Devendra Banhart</a>: <i>Für Hildegard von Bingen</i>, single from the 2013 album <i><a href="/wiki/Mala_(Devendra_Banhart_album)" title="Mala (Devendra Banhart album)">Mala</a></i> .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gordon_Hamilton_(composer)" title="Gordon Hamilton (composer)">Gordon Hamilton</a>: <i>The Trillion Souls</i> quotes Hildegard's <i>O Ignee Spiritus</i> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>The artwork <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dinner_Party" title="The Dinner Party">The Dinner Party</a></i> features a place setting for Hildegard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>In space, the <a href="/wiki/Minor_planet" title="Minor planet">minor planet</a> <a href="/wiki/898_Hildegard" title="898 Hildegard">898 Hildegard</a> is named for her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>In film, Hildegard has been portrayed by <a href="/wiki/Patricia_Routledge" title="Patricia Routledge">Patricia Routledge</a> in a BBC documentary called <i>Hildegard of Bingen</i> (1994),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> by <a href="/wiki/%C3%81ngela_Molina" title="Ángela Molina">Ángela Molina</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Barbarossa_(film)" title="Barbarossa (film)">Barbarossa</a></i> (2009)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RAI_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-RAI-99">[99]</a></sup> and by <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Sukowa" title="Barbara Sukowa">Barbara Sukowa</a> in the film <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vision_%E2%80%93_From_the_Life_of_Hildegard_von_Bingen" title="Vision – From the Life of Hildegard von Bingen">Vision</a></i>, directed by <a href="/wiki/Margarethe_von_Trotta" title="Margarethe von Trotta">Margarethe von Trotta</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>Hildegard was the subject of a 2012 fictionalized biographic novel "<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Illuminations_(novel)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Illuminations (novel) (page does not exist)">Illuminations</a>" by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Mary_Sharratt&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Mary Sharratt (page does not exist)">Mary Sharratt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>The plant genus <i><a href="/wiki/Hildegardia_(plant)" title="Hildegardia (plant)">Hildegardia</a></i> is named after her because of her contributions to herbal medicine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>A feature documentary film, "The Unruly Mystic: Saint Hildegard," was released by American director Michael M. Conti in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>

<li><i>Epistolarium pars secunda XCI-CCLr</i> edited by L. Van Acker, <a href="/wiki/Corpus_Christianorum" title="Corpus Christianorum">Corpus Christianorum</a> Continuatio Mediaevalis CCCM 91A (Turnhout: Brepols, 1993)</li>

<li><i>Liber divinorum operum</i>. A. Derolez and P. Dronke eds., Corpus Christianorum Continuatio Mediaevalis CCCM 92 (Turnhout: Brepols, 1996)</li>
<li>Hildegard of Bingen, <i>Two Hagiographies: Vita sancti Rupperti confessoris, Vita sancti Dysibodi episcopi,</i> ed. and trans. Hugh Feiss &amp; Christopher P. Evans, <a href="/wiki/Dallas_Medieval_Texts_and_Translations" title="Dallas Medieval Texts and Translations">Dallas Medieval Texts and Translations</a> 11 (Leuven and Paris: Peeters, 2010)</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=GkuK53bU4_UC" rel="nofollow"><i>Hildegard of Bingen's Unknown Language: An Edition, Translation, and Discussion</i></a>, ed. <li id="cite_note-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-30">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Critical editions of all three of Hildegard's major works have appeared in the <i>Corpus Christianorum: Continuatio Medievalis</i>: <i>Scivias</i> in vols. 43-43A, <i>Liber vitae meritorum</i> in vol. 90, and <i>Liber divinorum operum</i> in vol. 92.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-31">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Ferrante, Joan. "Correspondent: 'Blessed Is the Speech of Your Mouth'", in <i>Voice of the Living Light: Hildegard of Bingen and Her World</i>, ed. Barbara Newman (Berkeley: University of California Press, 1998), pp. 91-109. The modern critical edition (vols. 91-91b in the Corpus Christianorum: Continuatio Medievalis) by L. Van Acker and M. Klaes-Hachmöller lists 390 canonical letters along with 13 letters that appear in different forms in secondary manuscripts. The letters have been translated into English in three volumes: <i>The Letters of Hildegard of Bingen</i>, trans. Joseph L. Baird and Radd K. Ehrman (Oxford University Press, 1994, 1998, and 2004).</span></li>

<li>Ulrich, Ingeborg. <i>Hildegard of Bingen: Mystic, Healer, Companion of the Angels</i>. Trans. Linda M. Maloney. Collegeville, MN: Liturgical Press, 1993.</li>
<li>Ward, Benedicta. <i>Miracles and the Medieval Mind</i>. Philadelphia: University of Pennsylvania, 1987.</li>
<li>Weeks, Andrew. <i>German mysticism from Hildegard of Bingen to Ludwig Wittgenstein: a literary and intellectual history</i>. Albany: State University of New York Press, 1993. <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0791414191">ISBN 0-7914-1419-1</a></li>

<th class="navbox-group" scope="row" style="background-color:gold"><a href="/wiki/Catholic_theology" title="Catholic theology">Theology</a></th>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-odd" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px">
